					<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Honey</span></h1>

				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Sweet and viscous substance made by bees</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Honey_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Honey (disambiguation)">Honey (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner span:not(.skin-invert-image):not(.skin-invert):not(.bg-transparent) img{background-color:white}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner span:not(.skin-invert-image):not(.skin-invert):not(.bg-transparent) img{background-color:white}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Runny_hunny.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Runny_hunny.jpg/250px-Runny_hunny.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="379" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Runny_hunny.jpg/500px-Runny_hunny.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1780" data-file-height="2700" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">A jar of honey with a <a href="/wiki/Honey_dipper" title="Honey dipper">honey dipper</a> and an <a href="/wiki/American_biscuit" class="mw-redirect" title="American biscuit">American biscuit</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Honey</b> is a sweet and <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscous</a> substance made by several species of <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, the best-known of which are <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bees</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crane1990&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crane1990-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Grüter&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grüter-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey is made and stored to nourish bee colonies. Bees produce honey by gathering and then refining the <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugary</a> secretions of plants (primarily floral <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a>) or the secretions of other insects, like the <a href="/wiki/Honeydew_(secretion)" title="Honeydew (secretion)">honeydew</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a>. This refinement takes place both within individual bees, through <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymatic</a> activity, and during storage in the hive, through water evaporation that concentrates the honey's sugars until it is thick and viscous.
</p><p>Honey bees stockpile honey in the <a href="/wiki/Beehive" title="Beehive">hive</a>. Within the hive is a structure made from wax called <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb" title="Honeycomb">honeycomb</a>. The honeycomb is made up of hundreds or thousands of <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagonal</a> cells, into which the bees <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitate</a> honey for storage. Other honey-producing species of bee store the substance in different structures, such as the pots made of wax and <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resin</a> used by the <a href="/wiki/Stingless_bee" title="Stingless bee">stingless bee</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crane1990&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crane1990-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Grüter&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grüter-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Honey for human consumption is collected from wild bee colonies, or from the <a href="/wiki/Beehive" title="Beehive">hives</a> of domesticated bees. The honey produced by honey bees is the most familiar to humans, thanks to its worldwide commercial production and availability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-crane1999&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crane1999-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">husbandry</a> of bees is known as <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">beekeeping</a> or apiculture, with the cultivation of stingless bees usually referred to as <a href="/wiki/Meliponiculture" title="Meliponiculture">meliponiculture</a>.
</p><p>Honey is sweet because of its high concentrations of the <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>. It has about the same relative <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">sweetness</a> as <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> (table sugar).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NHB&#95;carbs&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHB_carbs-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Oregon&#95;State&#95;University&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oregon_State_University-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One standard <a href="/wiki/Tablespoon" title="Tablespoon">tablespoon</a> (14&#160;mL) of honey provides around 180 kilojoules (43 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">kilocalories</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a>. It has attractive chemical properties for <a href="/wiki/Baking" title="Baking">baking</a> and a distinctive flavor when used as a sweetener.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NHB&#95;carbs&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHB_carbs-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to honey's high sugar concentration and acidic <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> level many <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> cannot grow in it and, when properly stored, honey therefore does not <a href="/wiki/Food_spoilage" title="Food spoilage">spoil</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mundo&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundo-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Samples of honey discovered in <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> contexts have proven edible even after millennia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geiling&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geiling-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Prescott&#95;1999&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prescott_1999-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Miels_d%27Alsace_agriculture_biologique.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Miels_d%27Alsace_agriculture_biologique.jpg/250px-Miels_d%27Alsace_agriculture_biologique.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="193" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Miels_d%27Alsace_agriculture_biologique.jpg/500px-Miels_d%27Alsace_agriculture_biologique.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3046" data-file-height="2353" /></a><figcaption>French honey from different floral sources, with visible differences in color and texture</figcaption></figure>
<p>Honey use and production has a long and varied history, with its beginnings in <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistoric times</a>. Several cave paintings in <a href="/wiki/Cuevas_de_la_Ara%C3%B1a" title="Cuevas de la Araña">Cuevas de la Araña</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> depict humans foraging for honey at least 8,000 years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hunt&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crane83&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crane83-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Western_honey_bee" title="Western honey bee"><i>Apis mellifera</i></a> is an <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insect</a>, large-scale meliponiculture of <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> stingless bees has been practiced by <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayans</a> since <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> times.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Grüter&#95;2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grüter-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Quezada-Euan&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quezada-Euan-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" /></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Formation">Formation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Formation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bee_on_-calyx_935.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Bee_on_-calyx_935.jpg/250px-Bee_on_-calyx_935.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="196" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Bee_on_-calyx_935.jpg/500px-Bee_on_-calyx_935.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="548" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bee</a> with its proboscis extended into a <a href="/wiki/Calyx_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Calyx (botany)">calyx</a> of <a href="/wiki/Goldenrod" title="Goldenrod">goldenrod</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Honey_comb.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Honey_comb.jpg/250px-Honey_comb.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Honey_comb.jpg/500px-Honey_comb.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Honeycomb" title="Honeycomb">Honeycomb</a> displaying <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_prismatic_honeycomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hexagonal prismatic honeycomb">hexagonal prismatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Beeswax" title="Beeswax">wax</a> cells in which <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bees</a> store honey</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="By_honey_bees">By honey bees</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: By honey bees"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey is produced by bees who have collected <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Honeydew_(secretion)" title="Honeydew (secretion)">honeydew</a>. Bees value honey for its sugars, which they consume to support general <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic activity</a>, especially that of their flight muscles during <a href="/wiki/Forage_(honey_bee)" title="Forage (honey bee)">foraging</a>, and as a food for their <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a>. To this end bees stockpile honey to provide for themselves during ordinary foraging as well as during lean periods, as in <a href="/wiki/Overwintering" title="Overwintering">overwintering</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suarez&#95;Lighton&#95;Joos&#95;Roberts&#95;1996&#95;pp.&#95;12616–12620&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suarez_Lighton_Joos_Roberts_1996_pp._12616–12620-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NHB&#95;Bee&#95;facts&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHB_Bee_facts-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During foraging bees use part of the nectar they collect to power their flight muscles. The majority of nectar collected is not used to directly nourish the insects but is instead destined for <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_catalysis" title="Enzyme catalysis">enzymatic digestion</a>, and finally long-term <a href="/wiki/Food_storage" title="Food storage">storage</a> as honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suarez&#95;Lighton&#95;Joos&#95;Roberts&#95;1996&#95;pp.&#95;12616–12620&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suarez_Lighton_Joos_Roberts_1996_pp._12616–12620-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-cd&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cd-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During cold weather or when other food sources are scarce, adult and <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larval</a> bees consume stored honey, which is many times more energy-dense as the nectar from which it is made.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NHB&#95;Bee&#95;facts&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHB_Bee_facts-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After leaving the hive, a foraging bee collects sugar-rich nectar or honeydew. Nectar from the flower generally has a water content of 70 to 80% and is much less viscous than finished honey, which usually has a water content around 18%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-South&#95;Mountain&#95;Bees&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Mountain_Bees-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The water content of honeydew from aphids and other <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">true bugs</a> is generally very close to the sap on which those insects feed and is usually somewhat more dilute than nectar. One source describes the water content of honeydew as around 89%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whether it is feeding on nectar or honeydew, the bee sucks these runny fluids through its <a href="/wiki/Proboscis" title="Proboscis">proboscis</a>, which delivers the liquid to the bee's honey stomach or "honey crop".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cd&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cd-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This cavity lies just above its food stomach, the latter of which digests pollen and sugars consumed by an individual honey bee for its own nourishment.
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Western_honey_bee" title="Western honey bee">Apis mellifera</a></i>, the honey stomach holds about 40&#160;mg of liquid. This is about half the weight of an unladen bee. Collecting this quantity in nectar can require visits to more than one thousand flowers. When nectar is plentiful, it can take a bee more than an hour of ceaseless work to collect enough nectar to fill its honey crop. <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">Salivary</a> enzymes and proteins from the bee's <a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts#Hypopharynx" title="Insect mouthparts">hypopharyngeal gland</a> are secreted into the nectar once it is in the bee's honey stomach. These <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">substances</a> begin <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">cleaving</a> <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">complex sugars</a> like <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starches</a> into simpler sugars such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>. This process slightly raises the water content and the acidity of the partially digested nectar.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suarez&#95;Lighton&#95;Joos&#95;Roberts&#95;1996&#95;pp.&#95;12616–12620&#95;13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suarez_Lighton_Joos_Roberts_1996_pp._12616–12620-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rossano&#95;Larocca&#95;Polito&#95;Perna&#95;2012&#95;p.&#95;e49164&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rossano_Larocca_Polito_Perna_2012_p._e49164-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Once filled, the forager bees return to the hive. There they regurgitate and transfer nectar to hive bees. Once it is in their own honey stomachs, the hive bees regurgitate the nectar, repeatedly forming bubbles between their <a href="/wiki/Mandible_(insect_mouthpart)" title="Mandible (insect mouthpart)">mandibles</a>, speeding its digestion and concentration. These bubbles create a large surface area per volume and by this means the bees evaporate a portion of the nectar's water into the warm air of the hive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suarez&#95;Lighton&#95;Joos&#95;Roberts&#95;1996&#95;pp.&#95;12616–12620&#95;13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suarez_Lighton_Joos_Roberts_1996_pp._12616–12620-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-cd&#95;15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cd-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Hive bees form honey-processing groups. These groups work in relay, with one bee subjecting the processed nectar to bubbling and then passing the refined liquid on to others. It can take as long as 20 minutes of continuous regurgitation, digestion and evaporation until the product reaches storage quality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cd&#95;15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cd-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The new honey is then placed in honeycomb cells, which are left uncapped. This honey still has a very high water content, up to 70%, depending on the concentration of nectar gathered. At this stage of its refinement the water content of the honey is high enough that ubiquitous <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a> can <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">reproduce</a> in it, a process which, if left unchecked, would rapidly <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">consume</a> the new honey's sugars.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To combat this, bees use an ability rare among insects: the <a href="/wiki/Endogeny_(biology)" title="Endogeny (biology)">endogenous</a> generation of heat.
</p><p>Bees are among the few insects that can create large amounts of body heat. They use this ability to produce a constant ambient temperature in their hives. Hive temperatures are usually around 35&#160;°C (95&#160;°F) in the honey-storage areas. This temperature is regulated either by generating heat with their bodies or removing it through water evaporation. The evaporation removes water from the stored honey, drawing heat from the colony. The bees use their wings to govern hive cooling. Coordinated wing beating moves air across the wet honey, drawing out water and heat. Ventilation of the hive eventually expels both excess water and heat into the outside world.
</p><p>The process of evaporating continues until the honey reaches its final water content of between 15.5% and 18%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-South&#95;Mountain&#95;Bees&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Mountain_Bees-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This concentrates the sugars far beyond the <a href="/wiki/Solubility#Factors_affecting_solubility" title="Solubility">saturation point</a> of water, which is to say there is far more sugar dissolved in what little water remains in honey than ever could be dissolved in an equivalent volume of water. Honey, even at hive temperatures, is therefore a <a href="/wiki/Supercooling" title="Supercooling">supercooled</a> solution of various sugars in water. These concentrations of sugar can only be achieved near room temperature by evaporation of a less concentrated solution, in this case nectar. For <a href="/wiki/Osmotic_pressure" title="Osmotic pressure">osmotic reasons</a> such high concentrations of sugar are extremely unfavorable to microbiological reproduction and all <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> is consequently halted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NHB&#95;Bee&#95;facts&#95;14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHB_Bee_facts-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-cd&#95;15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cd-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The bees then cap the cells of finished honey with wax. This seals them from contamination and prevents further evaporation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cd&#95;15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cd-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>So long as its water concentration does not rise much above 18%, honey has an indefinite shelf life, both within the hive and after its removal by a <a href="/wiki/Beekeeper" title="Beekeeper">beekeeper</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NHB&#95;Bee&#95;facts&#95;14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHB_Bee_facts-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="By_other_insects">By other insects</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: By other insects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey bees are not the only <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">eusocial insects</a> to produce honey. All non-parasitic <a href="/wiki/Bumblebee" title="Bumblebee">bumblebees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stingless_bee" title="Stingless bee">stingless bees</a> produce honey. Some wasp species, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Brachygastra_lecheguana" title="Brachygastra lecheguana">Brachygastra lecheguana</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Brachygastra_mellifica" title="Brachygastra mellifica">Brachygastra mellifica</a>,</i> found in South and Central America, are known to feed on nectar and produce honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other wasps, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Polistes_versicolor" title="Polistes versicolor">Polistes versicolor</a></i>, also consume honey. In the middle of their life cycles they alternate between feeding on protein-rich pollen and feeding on honey, which is a far <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">denser</a> source of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Human_intervention">Human intervention</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Human intervention"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Humans have <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">semi-domesticated</a> several species of honey bee by taking advantage of their swarming stage. Swarming is the means by which new colonies are established when there is no longer space for expansion in the colony's present hive. The old queen lays eggs that will develop into new queens and then leads as many as half the colony to a site for a new hive. Bees generally swarm before a suitable location for another hive has been discovered by scouts sent out for this purpose. Until such a location is found the swarm will simply conglomerate near the former hive, often from tree branches. These swarms are unusually docile and amenable to transport by humans. When provided with a suitable nesting site, such as a commercial <a href="/wiki/Langstroth_hive" title="Langstroth hive">Langstroth hive</a>, the swarm will readily form a new colony in artificial surroundings. These semi-domesticated colonies are then looked after by humans practicing apiculture or meliponiculture. Captured bees are encouraged to forage, often in agricultural settings such as orchards, where pollinators are highly valued. The honey, <a href="/wiki/Bee_pollen" title="Bee pollen">pollen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beeswax" title="Beeswax">wax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plant_defense_against_herbivory" title="Plant defense against herbivory">resins</a> the bees produce are all harvested by humans for a variety of uses.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The term "semi-domesticated" is preferred because all bee colonies, even those in very large agricultural apiculture operations, readily leave the protection of humans in swarms that can establish successful wild colonies. Much of the effort in commercial beekeeping is dedicated to persuading a hive that is ready to swarm to produce more honeycomb in its present location. This is usually done by adding more space to the colony with <i><a href="/wiki/Honey_super" title="Honey super">honey supers</a></i>, empty boxes placed on top of an existing colony. The bees can then usually be enticed to develop this empty space instead of dividing their colony through swarming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Production">Production</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Production"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Collection">Collection</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Collection"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sealed_Honey_in_frame.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Sealed_Honey_in_frame.JPG/250px-Sealed_Honey_in_frame.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Sealed_Honey_in_frame.JPG/500px-Sealed_Honey_in_frame.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a><figcaption>Sealed frame of honey</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Extractor_Beekeeping.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Extractor_Beekeeping.jpg/250px-Extractor_Beekeeping.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="185" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Extractor_Beekeeping.jpg/500px-Extractor_Beekeeping.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>Extraction from a honeycomb</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Filtering_of_honey.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Filtering_of_honey.jpg/250px-Filtering_of_honey.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="282" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Filtering_of_honey.jpg/330px-Filtering_of_honey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Filtering_of_honey.jpg/500px-Filtering_of_honey.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1908" data-file-height="2836" /></a><figcaption>Filtering from a honeycomb</figcaption></figure>
<p>Honey is collected from wild bee colonies or from domesticated beehives. On average, a hive will produce about 29 kilograms (65&#160;lb) of honey per year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Wild bee nests are sometimes located by following a <a href="/wiki/Honeyguide" title="Honeyguide">honeyguide</a> bird.
</p><p>To safely collect honey from a hive, beekeepers typically pacify the bees using a <a href="/wiki/Bee_smoker" title="Bee smoker">bee smoker</a>. The smoke triggers a feeding instinct (an attempt to save the resources of the hive from a possible fire), making them less aggressive, and obscures the pheromones the bees use to communicate. The honeycomb is removed from the hive and the honey may be extracted from it either by crushing or by using a <a href="/wiki/Honey_extractor" title="Honey extractor">honey extractor</a>. The honey is then usually filtered to remove beeswax and other debris.
</p><p>Before the invention of removable frames, bee colonies were often sacrificed to conduct the harvest. The harvester would take all the available honey and replace the entire colony the next spring. Since the invention of removable frames, the principles of husbandry led most beekeepers to ensure that their bees have enough stores to survive the winter, either by leaving some honey in the beehive or by providing the colony with a honey substitute such as sugar water or crystalline sugar (often in the form of a "candyboard"). The amount of food necessary to survive the winter depends on the variety of bees and on the length and severity of local winters.
</p><p>Many animal species are attracted to wild or domestic sources of honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Preservation">Preservation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Preservation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Because of its composition and chemical properties, honey is suitable for long-term storage, and is easily assimilated even after long preservation. Honey, and objects immersed in honey, have been preserved for centuries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (However, no edible honey has been found in Egyptian tombs; all such cases have been proven to be other substances or only chemical traces.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) The key to preservation is limiting access to humidity. In its cured state, honey has a sufficiently high sugar content to inhibit fermentation. If exposed to moist air, its <a href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a> properties pull moisture into the honey, eventually diluting it to the point that fermentation can begin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bee&#95;Culture&#39;&#95;Page&#95;348&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bee_Culture&#39;_Page_348-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The long <a href="/wiki/Shelf_life" title="Shelf life">shelf life</a> of honey is attributed to an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> found in the stomach of bees. The bees mix <a href="/wiki/Glucose_oxidase" title="Glucose oxidase">glucose oxidase</a> with expelled nectar they previously consumed, creating two <a href="/wiki/Byproduct" class="mw-redirect" title="Byproduct">byproducts</a> – <a href="/wiki/Gluconic_acid" title="Gluconic acid">gluconic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, which are partially responsible for honey acidity and suppression of bacterial growth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geiling&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geiling-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Adulteration">Adulteration</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Adulteration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey is sometimes <a href="/wiki/Adulterant" title="Adulterant">adulterated</a> by the addition of other sugars, syrups or compounds. This may be done to alter flavour or viscosity, reduce production costs, or increase fructose content, which can inhibit <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallization</a>.
</p><p>Adulteration of honey has been documented since ancient times. Historical sources describe honey being blended with plant syrups such as <a href="/wiki/Maple_syrup" title="Maple syrup">maple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birch_syrup" title="Birch syrup">birch</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sorghum_syrup" class="mw-redirect" title="Sorghum syrup">sorghum</a> syrup and sold as pure honey. In some cases, crystallized honey was mixed with flour or other fillers. This practice could conceal adulteration until the honey was heated and liquefied.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wilson-2004&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2004-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In more recent times, clear, nearly flavourless <a href="/wiki/Corn_syrup" title="Corn syrup">corn syrup</a> has become the most common adulterant. Honey adulterated with corn syrup can be difficult to distinguish from unadulterated honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wilson-2004&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2004-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Alimentarius" title="Codex Alimentarius">Codex Alimentarius</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, products labelled as “honey” or “pure honey” must not be adulterated. Honey labelling requirements, however, vary between countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the National Honey Board identifies honey authenticity as a major challenge for the honey industry. Over the past half century, a range of analytical methods has been developed to detect food fraud. The National Honey Board notes that no single universal method is currently capable of detecting all forms of honey adulteration with adequate sensitivity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nhb&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhb-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One technique used to detect adulteration is <a href="/wiki/Isotope_ratio_mass_spectrometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Isotope ratio mass spectrometry">Isotope ratio mass spectrometry</a>. This method can identify the addition of <a href="/wiki/Cane_sugar" class="mw-redirect" title="Cane sugar">cane sugar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Corn_syrup" title="Corn syrup">corn syrup</a> by analysing carbon <a href="/wiki/Isotopic_signature" title="Isotopic signature">isotopic signatures</a>. Corn and sugar cane are <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 plants</a>, whereas the plants used by bees to produce honey, as well as <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beet</a>, are predominantly <a href="/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation" title="C3 carbon fixation">C3 plants</a>. Sugars derived from C4 plants alter the isotopic ratio of sugars in honey but do not affect the isotopic ratio of proteins. In unadulterated honey, the carbon isotopic ratios of sugars and proteins are expected to match. Using this method, adulteration levels as low as seven per cent can be detected.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nhb&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhb-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
