<p>In 2023, world production of honey was 1.9<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by China with 24% of the total, and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> as secondary producers (table).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Modern_uses">Modern uses</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Modern uses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Food">Food</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Food"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mellivory" title="Mellivory">Mellivory</a></div>
<p>Over its history as a food,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hunt&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the main uses of honey are in cooking, baking, desserts, as a spread on bread, as an addition to various beverages such as tea, and as a sweetener in some commercial beverages.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Due to its energy density, honey is an important food for virtually all <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> cultures in warm climates, with the <a href="/wiki/Hadza_people" title="Hadza people">Hadza people</a> ranking honey as their favorite food.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Honey_hunting" title="Honey hunting">Honey hunters</a> in Africa have a <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualistic</a> relationship with certain species of <a href="/wiki/Honeyguide" title="Honeyguide">honeyguide</a> birds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Fermentation">Fermentation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fermentation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Possibly the world's oldest <a href="/wiki/Fermented_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermented beverage">fermented beverage</a>, dating from 9,000 years ago,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mead" title="Mead">mead</a> ("honey wine") is the alcoholic product made by adding <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> to honey-water <a href="/wiki/Must" title="Must">must</a> and fermenting it for weeks or months.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jib&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jib-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-mol&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mol-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The yeast <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i> is commonly used in modern mead production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jib&#95;40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jib-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-mol&#95;41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mol-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Mead varieties include drinks called <a href="/wiki/Metheglin" class="mw-redirect" title="Metheglin">metheglin</a> (with spices or herbs), <a href="/wiki/Melomel" class="mw-redirect" title="Melomel">melomel</a> (with fruit juices, such as grape, specifically called <a href="/wiki/Pyment" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyment">pyment</a>), <a href="/wiki/Hippocras" title="Hippocras">hippocras</a> (with <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a>), and sack mead (high concentration of honey),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mol&#95;41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mol-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> many of which have been developed as commercial products numbering in the hundreds in the United States.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tierney&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tierney-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey is also used to make mead <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, called "braggot".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Physical_and_chemical_properties">Physical and chemical properties</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Physical and chemical properties"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystallized_honey_with_close-up.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Crystallized_honey_with_close-up.jpg/250px-Crystallized_honey_with_close-up.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="196" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Crystallized_honey_with_close-up.jpg/500px-Crystallized_honey_with_close-up.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3217" data-file-height="2521" /></a><figcaption>Crystallized honey: The inset shows a close-up of the honey, showing the individual glucose grains in the fructose mixture.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The physical properties of honey vary, depending on water content, the type of flora used to produce it (pasturage), temperature, and the proportion of the specific sugars it contains. Fresh honey is a <a href="/wiki/Supersaturation" title="Supersaturation">supersaturated</a> liquid, containing more sugar than the water can typically dissolve at ambient temperatures. At room temperature, honey is a <a href="/wiki/Supercooling" title="Supercooling">supercooled</a> liquid, in which the glucose precipitates into solid granules. This forms a semisolid solution of <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitated</a> glucose <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a> in a solution of fructose and other ingredients.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of honey typically ranges between 1.38 and 1.45&#160;kg/L at 20&#160;°C.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tomasik2003&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tomasik2003-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Phase_transitions">Phase transitions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Phase transitions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The melting point of crystallized honey is between 40 and 50&#160;°C (104 and 122&#160;°F), depending on its composition. Below this temperature, honey can be either in a <a href="/wiki/Metastable" class="mw-redirect" title="Metastable">metastable</a> state, meaning that it will not crystallize until a <a href="/wiki/Seed_crystal" title="Seed crystal">seed crystal</a> is added, or, more often, it is in a "labile" state, being saturated with enough sugars to crystallize spontaneously.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The rate of crystallization is affected by many factors, but the primary factor is the ratio of the main sugars: fructose to glucose. Honeys that are supersaturated with a very high percentage of glucose, such as <a href="/wiki/Brassica" title="Brassica">brassica</a> honey, crystallize almost immediately after harvesting, while honeys with a low percentage of glucose, such as chestnut or <a href="/wiki/Tupelo_(tree)" title="Tupelo (tree)">tupelo</a> honey, do not crystallize. Some types of honey may produce few but very large crystals, while others produce many small crystals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Piotr&#95;Tomasik&#95;p.&#95;74&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piotr_Tomasik_p._74-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Crystallization is also affected by water content, because a high percentage of water inhibits crystallization, as does a high <a href="/wiki/Dextrin" title="Dextrin">dextrin</a> content. Temperature also affects the rate of crystallization, with the fastest growth occurring between 13 and 17&#160;°C (55 and 63&#160;°F). Crystal nuclei (seeds) tend to form more readily if the honey is disturbed, by stirring, shaking, or agitating, rather than if left at rest. However, the <a href="/wiki/Nucleation" title="Nucleation">nucleation</a> of microscopic seed-crystals is greatest between 5 and 8&#160;°C (41 and 46&#160;°F). Therefore, larger but fewer crystals tend to form at higher temperatures, while smaller but more-numerous crystals usually form at lower temperatures. Below 5&#160;°C, the honey will not crystallize, thus the original texture and flavor can be preserved indefinitely.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Piotr&#95;Tomasik&#95;p.&#95;74&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piotr_Tomasik_p._74-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Honey is a supercooled liquid when stored below its melting point, as is normal. At very low temperatures, honey does not freeze solid; rather its viscosity increases. Like most <a href="/wiki/Viscous_liquid" title="Viscous liquid">viscous liquids</a>, the honey becomes thick and sluggish with decreasing temperature. At −20&#160;°C (−4&#160;°F), honey may appear or even feel solid, but it continues to flow at very low rates. Honey has a <a href="/wiki/Glass_transition" title="Glass transition">glass transition</a> between −42 and −51&#160;°C (−44 and −60&#160;°F). Below this temperature, honey enters a <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glassy</a> state and becomes an <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_solid" title="Amorphous solid">amorphous solid</a> (noncrystalline).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kántor&#95;Pitsi&#95;Thoen&#95;1999&#95;pp.&#95;2327–2330&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kántor_Pitsi_Thoen_1999_pp._2327–2330-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Rheology">Rheology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Rheology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Honey-miel.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Honey-miel.jpg/250px-Honey-miel.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="204" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Honey-miel.jpg/416px-Honey-miel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Honey-miel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="416" data-file-height="339" /></a><figcaption>Pouring raw honey. The sheet-like appearance of the flow is the result of high viscosity and low surface tension, contributing to the stickiness of honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> of honey is affected greatly by both temperature and water content. The higher the water percentage, the more easily honey <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">flows</a>. Above its melting point, however, water has little effect on viscosity. Aside from water content, the composition of most types of honey also has little effect on viscosity. At 25&#160;°C (77&#160;°F), honey with 14% water content generally has a viscosity around 400&#160;<a href="/wiki/Poise_(unit)" title="Poise (unit)">poise</a>, while a honey containing 20% water has a viscosity around 20&#160;poise. Viscosity increases very slowly with moderate cooling; a honey containing 16% water, at 70&#160;°C (158&#160;°F), has a viscosity around 2 poise, while at 30&#160;°C (86&#160;°F), the viscosity is around 70 poise. With further cooling, the increase in viscosity is more rapid, reaching 600 poise at around 14&#160;°C (57&#160;°F).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-bee-hexagon.net&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bee-hexagon.net-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, while honey is viscous, it has low <a href="/wiki/Surface_tension" title="Surface tension">surface tension</a> of 50–60 mJ/m<sup>2</sup>, making its <a href="/wiki/Wettability" class="mw-redirect" title="Wettability">wettability</a> similar to water, <a href="/wiki/Glycerin" class="mw-redirect" title="Glycerin">glycerin</a>, or most other liquids.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The high viscosity and wettability of honey cause <a href="/wiki/Adhesion" title="Adhesion">stickiness</a>, which is a time-dependent process in supercooled liquids between the glass-transition temperature (T<sub>g</sub>) and the crystalline-melting temperature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Most types of honey are <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_liquid" class="mw-redirect" title="Newtonian liquid">Newtonian liquids</a>, but a few types have <a href="/wiki/Non-Newtonian_fluid" title="Non-Newtonian fluid">non-Newtonian</a> viscous properties. Honeys from <a href="/wiki/Calluna_vulgaris" class="mw-redirect" title="Calluna vulgaris">heather</a> or <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81nuka" title="Mānuka">mānuka</a> display <a href="/wiki/Thixotropic" class="mw-redirect" title="Thixotropic">thixotropic</a> properties. These types of honey enter a gel-like state when motionless, but liquefy when stirred.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Electrical_and_optical_properties">Electrical and optical properties</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Electrical and optical properties"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Because honey contains <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a>, in the form of acids and minerals, it exhibits varying degrees of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>. Measurements of the electrical conductivity are used to determine the quality of honey in terms of <a href="/wiki/Ash_(analytical_chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ash (analytical chemistry)">ash</a> content.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bee-hexagon.net&#95;52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bee-hexagon.net-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The effect honey has on light is useful for determining the type and quality. Variations in its water content alter its <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a>. Water content can easily be measured with a <a href="/wiki/Refractometer" title="Refractometer">refractometer</a>. Typically, the refractive index for honey ranges from 1.504 at 13% water content to 1.474 at 25%. Honey also has an effect on <a href="/wiki/Polarized_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Polarized light">polarized light</a>, in that it rotates the polarization plane. The fructose gives a negative rotation, while the glucose gives a positive one. The overall rotation can be used to measure the ratio of the mixture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bee-hexagon.net&#95;52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bee-hexagon.net-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bee&#95;Culture&#39;&#95;Page&#95;348&#95;31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bee_Culture&#39;_Page_348-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey is generally pale yellow and dark brown in color,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> but other colors can occur, depending on the sugar source.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bee colonies that forage on <a href="/wiki/Kudzu" title="Kudzu">Kudzu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pueraria_montana_var._lobata" title="Pueraria montana var. lobata"><i>Pueraria montana</i> var. <i>lobata</i></a>) flowers, for example, produce honey that varies in color from red to purple.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Questionable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (November 2023)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Hygroscopy_and_fermentation">Hygroscopy and fermentation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hygroscopy and fermentation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey has the ability to absorb moisture directly from the air, a phenomenon called <a href="/wiki/Hygroscopy" title="Hygroscopy">hygroscopy</a>. The amount of water the honey absorbs is dependent on the relative humidity of the air. Because honey contains yeast, this hygroscopic nature requires that honey be stored in sealed containers to prevent fermentation, which usually begins if the honey's water content rises much above 25%. Honey tends to absorb more water in this manner than the individual sugars allow on their own, which may be due to other ingredients it contains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bee&#95;Culture&#39;&#95;Page&#95;348&#95;31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bee_Culture&#39;_Page_348-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fermentation of honey usually occurs after crystallization, because without the glucose, the liquid portion of the honey primarily consists of a concentrated mixture of fructose, acids, and water, providing the yeast with enough of an increase in the water percentage for growth. Honey that is to be stored at room temperature for long periods of time is often <a href="/wiki/Pasteurized" class="mw-redirect" title="Pasteurized">pasteurized</a>, to kill any yeast, by heating it above 70&#160;°C (158&#160;°F).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bee&#95;Culture&#39;&#95;Page&#95;348&#95;31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bee_Culture&#39;_Page_348-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Thermal_characteristics">Thermal characteristics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Thermal characteristics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Creamed_honey_Maillard_reaction_after_aging.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Creamed_honey_Maillard_reaction_after_aging.jpg/250px-Creamed_honey_Maillard_reaction_after_aging.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Creamed_honey_Maillard_reaction_after_aging.jpg/500px-Creamed_honey_Maillard_reaction_after_aging.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>Creamed honey: the honey on the left is fresh, and the honey on the right has been aged at room temperature for two years. The Maillard reaction produces considerable differences in the color and flavor of the aged honey, which remains edible.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Like all sugar compounds, honey <a href="/wiki/Caramelize" class="mw-redirect" title="Caramelize">caramelizes</a> if heated sufficiently, becoming darker in color, and eventually burns. However, honey contains fructose, which caramelizes at lower temperatures than glucose.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The temperature at which caramelization begins varies, depending on the composition, but is typically between 70 and 110&#160;°C (158 and 230&#160;°F). Honey also contains acids, which act as <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a> for caramelization. The specific types of acids and their amounts play a primary role in determining the exact temperature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Of these acids, the amino acids, which occur in very small amounts, play an important role in the darkening of honey. The amino acids form darkened compounds called <a href="/wiki/Melanoidin" title="Melanoidin">melanoidins</a>, during a <a href="/wiki/Maillard_reaction" title="Maillard reaction">Maillard reaction</a>. The Maillard reaction occurs slowly at room temperature, taking from a few to several months to show visible darkening, but speeds up dramatically with increasing temperatures. However, the reaction can also be slowed by storing the honey at colder temperatures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike many other liquids, honey has very poor <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of 0.5 W/(m⋅K) at 13% water content (compared to 401 W/(m⋅K) of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>), taking a long time to reach <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to its high <a href="/wiki/Kinematic_viscosity" class="mw-redirect" title="Kinematic viscosity">kinematic viscosity</a> honey does not transfer heat through momentum diffusion (<a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a>) but rather through <a href="/wiki/Heat_conduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat conduction">thermal diffusion</a> (more like a solid), so melting crystallized honey can easily result in localized caramelization if the heat source is too hot or not evenly distributed. However, honey takes substantially longer to liquefy when just above the melting point than at elevated temperatures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bee-hexagon.net&#95;52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bee-hexagon.net-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Melting 20&#160;kg (44&#160;lb) of crystallized honey at 40&#160;°C (104&#160;°F) can take up to 24 hours, while 50&#160;kg (110&#160;lb) may take twice as long. These times can be cut nearly in half by heating at 50&#160;°C (122&#160;°F); however, many of the minor substances in honey can be affected greatly by heating, changing the flavor, aroma, or other properties, so heating is usually done at the lowest temperature and for the shortest time possible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Acid_content_and_flavor_effects">Acid content and flavor effects</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Acid content and flavor effects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The average <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of honey is 3.9, but can range from 3.4 to 6.1.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nhbtech&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhbtech-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey contains many kinds of acids, both <a href="/wiki/Organic_acid" title="Organic acid">organic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino</a>. However, the different types and their amounts vary considerably, depending on the type of honey. These acids may be <a href="/wiki/Aromaticity" title="Aromaticity">aromatic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aliphatic_compound#Aliphatic_acids" title="Aliphatic compound">aliphatic</a> (nonaromatic). The aliphatic acids contribute greatly to the flavor of honey by interacting with the flavors of other ingredients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nhbtech&#95;63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhbtech-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Organic acids comprise most of the acids in honey, accounting for 0.17–1.17% of the mixture, with <a href="/wiki/Gluconic_acid" title="Gluconic acid">gluconic acid</a> formed by the actions of <a href="/wiki/Glucose_oxidase" title="Glucose oxidase">glucose oxidase</a> as the most prevalent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nhbtech&#95;63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhbtech-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Minor amounts of other organic acids are present, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butyric_acid" title="Butyric acid">butyric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malic_acid" title="Malic acid">malic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyroglutamic_acid" title="Pyroglutamic acid">pyroglutamic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propionic_acid" title="Propionic acid">propionic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valeric_acid" title="Valeric acid">valeric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capronic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Capronic acid">capronic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palmitic_acid" title="Palmitic acid">palmitic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Succinic_acid" title="Succinic acid">succinic</a>, among many others.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nhbtech&#95;63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhbtech-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Volatile_organic_compounds">Volatile organic compounds</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Volatile organic compounds"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Individual honeys from different plant sources contain over 100 <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a> (VOCs), which play a primary role in determining honey <a href="/wiki/Flavor_(taste)" class="mw-redirect" title="Flavor (taste)">flavors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Odor" title="Odor">aromas</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Patrignani&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patrignani-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-suarez&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suarez-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> VOCs are carbon-based compounds that readily <a href="/wiki/Vaporization" title="Vaporization">vaporize</a> into the air, providing aroma, including the scents of flowers, essential oils, or ripening fruit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-suarez&#95;67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suarez-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The typical chemical families of VOCs found in honey include <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">alcohols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Furan" title="Furan">furans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyran" title="Pyran">pyrans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norisoprenoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Norisoprenoid">norisoprenoids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a>, among many others and their derivatives.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-suarez&#95;67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suarez-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The specific VOCs and their amounts vary considerably between different types of honey obtained by bees foraging on different plant sources.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Patrignani&#95;66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patrignani-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-suarez&#95;67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suarez-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By example, when comparing the mixture of VOCs in different honeys in one review, <a href="/wiki/Longan" title="Longan">longan</a> honey had a higher amount of volatiles (48 VOCs), while <a href="/wiki/Sunflower" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a> honey had the lowest number of volatiles (8 VOCs).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>VOCs are primarily introduced into the honey from the nectar, where they are excreted by the flowers imparting individual scents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The specific types and concentrations of certain VOCs can be used to determine the type of flora used to produce monofloral honeys.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-suarez&#95;67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suarez-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The specific geography, soil composition and acidity used to grow the flora also have an effect on honey aroma properties,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Patrignani&#95;66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patrignani-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> such as a "fruity" or "grassy" aroma from longan honey, or a "waxy" aroma from sunflower honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dominant VOCs in one study were <a href="/wiki/Linalool" title="Linalool">linalool</a> <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a>, trans-linalool oxide, 2-<a href="/wiki/Phenylacetaldehyde" title="Phenylacetaldehyde">phenylacetaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benzyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Benzyl">benzyl</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isophorone" title="Isophorone">isophorone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Methyl">methyl</a> <a href="/wiki/Nonanoate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonanoate">nonanoate</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>VOCs can also be introduced from the bodies of the bees, be produced by the enzymatic actions of digestion, or from chemical reactions that occur between different substances within the honey during storage, and therefore may change, increase, or decrease over long periods of time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Patrignani&#95;66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patrignani-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> VOCs may be produced, altered, or greatly affected by temperature and processing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Patrignani&#95;66-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patrignani-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some VOCs are heat labile, and are destroyed at elevated temperatures, while others can be created during non-enzymatic reactions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Maillard_reaction" title="Maillard reaction">Maillard reaction</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-suarez&#95;67-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suarez-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> VOCs are responsible for nearly all of the aroma produced by a honey, which may be described as "sweet", "flowery", "citrus", "almond" or "rancid", among other terms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition, VOCs play a large role in determining the specific flavor of the honey, both through the aromas and flavor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> VOCs from honeys in different geographic regions can be used as floral markers of those regions, and as markers of the bees that foraged the nectars.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-patt&#95;65-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patt-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Patrignani&#95;66-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patrignani-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Classification">Classification</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey is classified by its source (floral or not), and divisions are made according to the packaging and processing used. <a href="/wiki/Regional_honeys" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional honeys">Regional honeys</a> are also identified. In the US, honey is also graded on its color and optical density by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> standards, graded on the Pfund scale, which ranges from 0 for "water white" honey to more than 114 for "dark amber" honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Plant_source">Plant source</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Plant source"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Generally, honey is classified by the floral source of the nectar from which it was made. Honeys can be from specific types of flower nectars or can be blended after collection. The pollen in honey is traceable to floral source and therefore region of origin. The <a href="/wiki/Rheology" title="Rheology">rheological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melissopalynology" title="Melissopalynology">melissopalynological</a> properties of honey can be used to identify the major plant nectar source used in its production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-honeyRheo&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honeyRheo-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Monofloral">Monofloral</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Monofloral"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Monofloral_honey" title="Monofloral honey">Monofloral honey</a> is made primarily from the nectar of one type of flower. Monofloral honeys have distinctive flavors and colors because of differences between their principal <a href="/wiki/Nectar_source" title="Nectar source">nectar sources</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-honeydefs&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honeydefs-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To produce monofloral honey, beekeepers keep beehives in an area where the bees have access, as far as possible, to only one type of flower. In practice, a small proportion of any monofloral honey will be from other flower types. Typical examples of North American monofloral honeys are <a href="/wiki/Clover" title="Clover">clover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orange_blossom" title="Orange blossom">orange blossom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salvia" title="Salvia">sage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tupelo_(tree)" title="Tupelo (tree)">tupelo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buckwheat" title="Buckwheat">buckwheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fireweed" class="mw-redirect" title="Fireweed">fireweed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesquite" title="Mesquite">mesquite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sourwood" class="mw-redirect" title="Sourwood">sourwood</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Honey&#95;Grading&#95;Manual&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honey_Grading_Manual-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cherry" title="Cherry">cherry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blueberry" title="Blueberry">blueberry</a>.
</p><p>Some typical European examples include <a href="/wiki/Thyme" title="Thyme">thyme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thistle" title="Thistle">thistle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ericaceae" title="Ericaceae">heather</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acacia" title="Acacia">acacia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dandelion" class="mw-redirect" title="Dandelion">dandelion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunflower" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lavender" class="mw-redirect" title="Lavender">lavender</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honeysuckle" title="Honeysuckle">honeysuckle</a>, and varieties from <a href="/wiki/Tilia" title="Tilia">lime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chestnut" title="Chestnut">chestnut</a> trees.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> (e.g. Egypt), examples include clover, <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">citrus</a> (mainly orange blossoms).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The unique flora of Australia yields a number of distinctive honeys, with some of the most popular being <a href="/wiki/Yellow_box" class="mw-redirect" title="Yellow box">yellow box</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_gum" title="Blue gum">blue gum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ironbark" title="Ironbark">ironbark</a>, bush <a href="/wiki/List_of_Eucalyptus_species" title="List of Eucalyptus species">mallee</a>, Tasmanian <a href="/wiki/Eucryphia_lucida" title="Eucryphia lucida">leatherwood</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Macadamia" title="Macadamia">macadamia</a>. <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81nuka_honey" title="Mānuka honey">Mānuka honey</a>, produced from the nectar of Leptospermum scoparium in New Zealand and parts of Australia, is darker in color and has an earthy, slightly bitter flavor profile compared with lighter honeys such as clover.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Farag2021&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farag2021-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Polyfloral">Polyfloral</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Polyfloral"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Polyfloral honey, also known as wildflower honey,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Honey&#95;Floral&#95;Source&#95;Guide&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honey_Floral_Source_Guide-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is derived from the nectar of many types of flowers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-honeydefs&#95;70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honeydefs-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-honeybook&#95;polyfloral&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honeybook_polyfloral-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The taste may vary from year to year, and the aroma and the flavor can be more or less intense, depending on which flowers are blooming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-honeydefs&#95;70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-honeydefs-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Honeydew_honey">Honeydew honey</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Honeydew honey"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honeydew honey is made from bees taking direct secretions from trees such as <a href="/wiki/Pinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinus">pine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abies" class="mw-redirect" title="Abies">fir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castanea" class="mw-redirect" title="Castanea">chestnut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quercus" class="mw-redirect" title="Quercus">oak</a> or primarily <a href="/wiki/Honeydew_(secretion)" title="Honeydew (secretion)">honeydew</a>, the sweet secretions of <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a> or other plant-sap-sucking insects, to produce honey rather than from <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pita2017&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pita2017-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This honey has a much larger proportion of indigestibles than light floral honeys, thus causing <a href="/wiki/Diseases_of_the_honey_bee#Dysentery" class="mw-redirect" title="Diseases of the honey bee">dysentery to the bees</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honeydew honey has a stronger and less sweet flavor than nectar-based honey, and European countries have been the primary market for honeydew honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pita2017&#95;75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pita2017-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Greece, <a href="/wiki/Pine_honey" title="Pine honey">pine honey</a>, a type of honeydew honey, constitutes 60–65% of honey production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Classification_by_packaging_and_processing">Classification by packaging and processing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Classification by packaging and processing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Texas_State_Fair_honey.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Texas_State_Fair_honey.jpg/250px-Texas_State_Fair_honey.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Texas_State_Fair_honey.jpg/500px-Texas_State_Fair_honey.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a><figcaption>A variety of honey flavors and container sizes and styles from the 2008 <a href="/wiki/Texas_State_Fair" class="mw-redirect" title="Texas State Fair">Texas State Fair</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Generally, honey is bottled in its familiar liquid form, but it is sold in other forms, and can be subjected to a variety of processing methods.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Crystallized honey</b> occurs when some of the glucose content has spontaneously crystallized from solution as the monohydrate. It is also called "granulated honey" or "candied honey". Honey that has crystallized (or is commercially purchased crystallized) can be returned to a liquid state by warming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Flottum2010&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flottum2010-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite a common misconception, honey crystallizing does not mean it has expired.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Pasteurized honey</b> has been heated in a <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">pasteurization</a> process which requires temperatures of 72&#160;°C (161&#160;°F) or higher. Pasteurization destroys yeast cells. It also liquefies any microcrystals in the honey, which delays the onset of visible crystallization. However, excessive heat exposure also results in product deterioration, as it increases the level of <a href="/wiki/Hydroxymethylfurfural" title="Hydroxymethylfurfural">hydroxymethylfurfural</a> (HMF)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Increases the level of something not even proven to be there? How about some citation of a scientifically and statistically relevant study proving both the existence of HMF in honey in any significant quantity AND its increase to significant or dangerous levels by heating? I.e. Onus probandi. (January 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and reduces enzyme (e.g. diastase) activity. Heat also darkens the honey, and affects taste and fragrance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Raw honey</b> is as it exists in the beehive or as obtained by extraction, settling, or straining, without adding heat (although some honey that has been "minimally processed" is often labeled as raw honey).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Raw honey contains some pollen and may contain small particles of wax.</li>
<li><b>Strained honey</b> has been passed through a mesh material to remove particulate material<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (pieces of wax, <a href="/wiki/Propolis" title="Propolis">propolis</a>, other defects) without removing pollen, minerals, or enzymes.</li>
<li><b>Filtered honey</b> of any type has been filtered to the extent that all or most of the fine particles, pollen grains, air bubbles, or other materials normally found in suspension, have been removed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The process typically heats honey to 66–77&#160;°C (150–170&#160;°F) to more easily pass through the filter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Damerow2011&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Damerow2011-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Filtered honey is very clear and will not crystallize as quickly,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Damerow2011&#95;86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Damerow2011-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> making it preferred by supermarkets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FRTWfB&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FRTWfB-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The most common method involves the addition of <a href="/wiki/Diatomaceous_earth" title="Diatomaceous earth">diatomaceous earth</a> to honey that is heated to 60&#160;°C (140&#160;°F) and passed through filter paper or canvas until a cake of diatomaceous earth builds up on the filter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Honey&#95;Grading&#95;Manual&#95;71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honey_Grading_Manual-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Ultrasonicated honey</b> has been processed by <a href="/wiki/Sonication" title="Sonication">ultrasonication</a>, a nonthermal processing alternative for honey. When honey is exposed to ultrasonication, most of the yeast cells are destroyed. Those cells that survive sonication generally lose their ability to grow, which reduces the rate of honey fermentation substantially. Ultrasonication also eliminates existing crystals and inhibits further crystallization in honey. Ultrasonically aided liquefaction can work at substantially lower temperatures around 35&#160;°C (95&#160;°F) and can reduce liquefaction time to less than 30 seconds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Creamed_honey" title="Creamed honey">Creamed honey</a></b>, also called whipped honey, spun honey, churned honey, honey fondant, and, in the UK, set honey, has been processed to control crystallization. Creamed honey contains a large number of small crystals, which prevent the formation of larger crystals that can occur in unprocessed honey. The processing also produces a honey with a smooth, spreadable consistency.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sharma2005&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma2005-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Dried honey</b> has the moisture extracted from liquid honey to create completely solid, nonsticky granules. This process may or may not include the use of drying and <a href="/wiki/Anticaking_agent" title="Anticaking agent">anticaking agents</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Krell1996&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krell1996-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dried honey is used in baked goods,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Krell1996&#95;90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krell1996-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and to garnish desserts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Comb_honey" title="Comb honey">Comb honey</a></b> is still in the honey bees' wax comb. It is traditionally collected using standard wooden <a href="/wiki/Hive_frame" title="Hive frame">frames</a> in <a href="/wiki/Honey_super" title="Honey super">honey supers</a>. The frames are collected and the comb is cut out in chunks before packaging. As an alternative to this labor-intensive method, plastic rings or cartridges can be used that do not require manual cutting of the comb, and speed packaging. Comb honey harvested in the traditional manner is also referred to as "cut-comb honey".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Flottum2010&#95;79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flottum2010-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 13">&#58;&#8202;13&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><b>Chunk honey</b> is packed in wide-mouthed containers; it consists of one or more pieces of comb honey immersed in extracted liquid honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Flottum2010&#95;79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flottum2010-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 13">&#58;&#8202;13&#8202;</span></sup></li>
<li><b>Honey decoctions</b> are made from honey or honey byproducts which have been dissolved in water, then reduced (usually by means of boiling). Other ingredients may then be added. (For example, <a href="/wiki/Abbamele" title="Abbamele">abbamele</a> has added citrus.) The resulting product may be similar to <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">molasses</a>.</li>
<li><b>Baker's honey</b> is outside the normal specification for honey, due to a "foreign" taste or odor, or because it has begun to ferment or has been overheated. It is generally used as an ingredient in food processing. Additional requirements exist for labeling baker's honey, including that it may not be sold labeled simply as "honey".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Grading">Grading</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Grading"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Food_grading" title="Food grading">Food grading</a></div>
<p>Countries have differing standards for grading honey.
In the US, honey grading is performed voluntarily based upon <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> standards. USDA offers inspection and grading "as on-line (in-plant) or lot inspection...upon application, on a fee-for-service basis." Honey is graded based upon a number of factors, including water content, flavor and aroma, absence of defects, and clarity. Honey is also classified by color, though it is not a factor in the grading scale.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The USDA honey grade scale is:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Grade
</th>
<th>Soluble solids
</th>
<th width="30%">Flavor and aroma
</th>
<th width="30%">Absence of defects
</th>
<th width="30%">Clarity
</th></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<th>A
</th>
<td>≥ 81.4%
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Good</dt>
<dd>"has a good, normal flavor and aroma for the predominant floral source or, when blended, a good flavor for the blend of floral sources and the honey is free from caramelized flavor or objectionable flavor caused by fermentation, smoke, chemicals, or other causes with the exception of the predominant floral source"</dd></dl>
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Practically free</dt>
<dd>"contains practically no defects that affect the appearance or edibility of the product"</dd></dl>
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Clear</dt>
<dd>"may contain air bubbles which do not materially affect the appearance of the product and may contain a trace of pollen grains or other finely divided particles of suspended material which do not affect the appearance of the product"</dd></dl>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<th>B
</th>
<td>≥ 81.4%
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Reasonably good</dt>
<dd>"has a reasonably good, normal flavor and aroma for the predominant floral source or, when blended, a reasonably good flavor for the blend of floral sources and the honey is practically free from caramelized flavor and is free from objectionable flavor caused by fermentation, smoke, chemicals, or other causes with the exception of the predominant floral source"</dd></dl>
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Reasonably free</dt>
<dd>"may contain defects which do not materially affect the appearance or edibility of the product"</dd></dl>
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Reasonably clear</dt>
<dd>"may contain air bubbles, pollen grains, or other finely divided particles of suspended material which do not materially affect the appearance of the product"</dd></dl>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<th>C
</th>
<td>≥ 80.0%
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Fairly good</dt>
<dd>"has a fairly good, normal flavor and aroma for the predominant floral source or, when blended, a fairly good flavor for the blend of floral sources and the honey is reasonably free from caramelized flavor and is free from objectionable flavor caused by fermentation, smoke, chemicals, or other causes with the exception of the predominant floral source"</dd></dl>
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Fairly free</dt>
<dd>"may contain defects which do not seriously affect the appearance or edibility of the product"</dd></dl>
</td>
<td>
<dl><dt>Fairly clear</dt>
<dd>"may contain air bubbles, pollen grains, or other finely divided particles of suspended material which do not seriously affect the appearance of the product"</dd></dl>

<p>India certifies honey grades based on additional factors, such as the Fiehe's test, and other empirical measurements.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Indicators_of_quality">Indicators of quality</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Indicators of quality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>High-quality honey can be distinguished by fragrance, taste, and consistency. Ripe, freshly collected, high-quality honey at 20&#160;°C (68&#160;°F) should flow from a knife in a straight stream, without breaking into separate drops.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Honey&#95;production&#95;96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honey_production-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After falling down, the honey should form a bead. The honey, when poured, should form small, temporary layers that disappear fairly quickly, indicating high viscosity. If not, it indicates honey with excessive water content of over 20%,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Honey&#95;production&#95;96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Honey_production-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> not suitable for long-term preservation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In jars, fresh honey should appear as a pure, consistent fluid, and should not set in layers. Within a few weeks to a few months of extraction, many varieties of honey crystallize into a cream-colored solid. Some varieties of honey, including tupelo, acacia, and sage, crystallize less regularly. Honey may be heated during bottling at temperatures of 40–49&#160;°C (104–120&#160;°F) to delay or inhibit crystallization. Overheating is indicated by change in enzyme levels, for instance, <a href="/wiki/Diastase" title="Diastase">diastase</a> activity, which can be determined with the Schade or the <a href="/wiki/Phadebas" title="Phadebas">Phadebas</a> methods. A fluffy film on the surface of the honey (like a white foam), or marble-colored or white-spotted crystallization on a container's sides, is formed by air bubbles trapped during the bottling process.
</p><p>A 2008 Italian study determined that <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy">nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy</a> can be used to distinguish between different honey types, and can be used to pinpoint the area where it was produced. Researchers were able to identify differences in acacia and polyfloral honeys by the differing proportions of fructose and sucrose, as well as differing levels of aromatic <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> <a href="/wiki/Phenylalanine" title="Phenylalanine">phenylalanine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">tyrosine</a>. This ability allows greater ease of selecting compatible stocks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Nutrition">Nutrition</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Nutrition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
