</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><hr /><div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://fdc.nal.usda.gov/food-details/169640/nutrients">Full Link to USDA Database entry</a></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below wrap" style="background:#e0e0e0; color:inherit;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal"><sup>†</sup>Percentages estimated using <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values" title="Reference Daily Intake">US&#160;recommendations</a> for adults,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FDADailyValues&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDADailyValues-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from the <a href="/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine" title="National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine">National Academies</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NationalAcademiesPotassium&#95;100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Honey is 17% water and 82% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, and has negligible content of <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, while containing no <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (table). Honey supplies no <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> in significant content (table). In a reference amount of 100&#160;g (3.5&#160;oz), honey supplies 300 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calories</a> (table).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sugar_profile">Sugar profile</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Sugar profile"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey is mainly <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> (41% of sugars) and <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> (36%) (table), with remaining sugars including <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maltose" title="Maltose">maltose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, each supplying 3% or less of total sugars (table, USDA reference).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Glycemic_index" title="Glycemic index">glycemic index</a> of honey has a possible range of 31–78, depending on the variety.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The specific composition, color, aroma, and flavor of any batch of honey depend on the flowers foraged by bees that produced the honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hunt&#95;10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Medical_use_and_research">Medical use and research</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Medical use and research"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Apitherapy" title="Apitherapy">Apitherapy</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Wounds_and_burns">Wounds and burns</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Wounds and burns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey is a <a href="/wiki/Folk_treatment" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk treatment">folk treatment</a> for burns and other skin injuries. Preliminary evidence suggests that it aids in the healing of partial thickness burns 4–5 days faster than other dressings, and moderate evidence suggests that post-operative infections treated with honey heal faster and with fewer adverse events than with <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gauze" title="Gauze">gauze</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jull2015&#95;102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jull2015-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The evidence for the use of honey in various other wound treatments is of low quality, and firm conclusions cannot be drawn.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jull2015&#95;102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jull2015-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Evidence does not support the use of honey-based products for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Venous_ulcer" title="Venous ulcer">venous stasis ulcers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ingrown_toenail" class="mw-redirect" title="Ingrown toenail">ingrown toenail</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-OMeara&#95;Al-Kurdi&#95;Ologun&#95;Ovington&#95;2014&#95;104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OMeara_Al-Kurdi_Ologun_Ovington_2014-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Eekhof&#95;Van&#95;Wijk&#95;Knuistingh&#95;Neven&#95;van&#95;der&#95;Wouden&#95;2012&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eekhof_Van_Wijk_Knuistingh_Neven_van_der_Wouden_2012-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several medical-grade honey products have been approved by the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a> <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> for use in treating minor wounds and burns.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Saikaly2017&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saikaly2017-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Antibiotic">Antibiotic</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Antibiotic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey has long been used as a <a href="/wiki/Topical_antibiotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Topical antibiotic">topical antibiotic</a> by practitioners of <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbal_medicine" title="Herbal medicine">herbal medicine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Buhner&#95;107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buhner-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Boukraa&#95;108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boukraa-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey's antibacterial effects were first demonstrated by the Dutch scientist Bernardus Adrianus van Ketel in 1892.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dustmann&#95;109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dustmann-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nolan2019&#95;110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nolan2019-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since then, numerous studies have shown that honey has broad-spectrum antibacterial activity against <a href="/wiki/Gram-positive_bacteria" title="Gram-positive bacteria">gram-positive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gram-negative_bacteria" title="Gram-negative bacteria">gram-negative</a> bacteria, although potency varies widely between different honeys.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Saikaly2017&#95;106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saikaly2017-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nolan2019&#95;110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nolan2019-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Molan2015&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Molan2015-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-future&#95;112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-future-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/List_of_antibiotic-resistant_bacteria" title="List of antibiotic-resistant bacteria">antibiotic-resistant bacteria</a> in the last few decades, there has been renewed interest in researching the antibacterial properties of honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Boukraa&#95;108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boukraa-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Components of honey under preliminary research for potential antibiotic use include <a href="/wiki/Methylglyoxal" title="Methylglyoxal">methylglyoxal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, and royalisin (also called defensin-1).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Alvarez2014&#95;115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez2014-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cough">Cough</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Cough"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>For chronic and acute coughs, a <a href="/wiki/Cochrane_(organisation)" title="Cochrane (organisation)">Cochrane</a> review found no strong evidence for or against the use of honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-lozenges&#95;116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lozenges-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Oduwole-2018&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oduwole-2018-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For treating children, the systematic review concluded with moderate to low evidence that honey helps more than no treatment, <a href="/wiki/Diphenhydramine" title="Diphenhydramine">diphenhydramine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a> at giving relief from coughing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Oduwole-2018&#95;117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oduwole-2018-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey does not appear to work better than <a href="/wiki/Dextromethorphan" title="Dextromethorphan">dextromethorphan</a> at relieving coughing in children.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Oduwole-2018&#95;117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oduwole-2018-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other reviews have also supported the use of honey for treating children.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Goldman2014&#95;118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman2014-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Paul2012&#95;119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paul2012-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The UK <a href="/wiki/Medicines_and_Healthcare_products_Regulatory_Agency" title="Medicines and Healthcare products Regulatory Agency">Medicines and Healthcare products Regulatory Agency</a> recommends avoiding giving <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-the-counter">over-the-counter</a> cough and <a href="/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a> medication to children under six, and suggests "a homemade remedy containing honey and lemon is likely to be just as useful and safer to take", but warns that honey should not be given to babies because of the risk of infant <a href="/wiki/Botulism" title="Botulism">botulism</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nhs-cough&#95;120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhs-cough-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The World Health Organization recommends honey as a treatment for coughs and sore throats, including for children, stating that no reason exists to believe it is less effective than a commercial remedy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other">Other</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Other"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The use of honey has been recommended as a temporary intervention for known or suspected <a href="/wiki/Button_cell" title="Button cell">button cell</a> battery ingestions to reduce the risk and severity of injury to the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a> caused by the battery prior to its removal.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mubarak&#95;Benninga&#95;Broekaert&#95;Dolinsek&#95;2021&#95;pp.&#95;129–136&#95;123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mubarak_Benninga_Broekaert_Dolinsek_2021_pp._129–136-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sethia&#95;Gibbs&#95;Jacobs&#95;Reilly&#95;2021&#95;pp.&#95;549–563&#95;124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sethia_Gibbs_Jacobs_Reilly_2021_pp._549–563-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is no evidence that honey is beneficial for treating <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mayo&#95;125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayo-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although honey may be useful for controlling <a href="/wiki/Side_effect" title="Side effect">side effects</a> of <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">radiation therapy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> used to treat cancer.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bardy&#95;Slevin&#95;Mais&#95;Molassiotis&#95;2008&#95;pp.&#95;2604–2623&#95;126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardy_Slevin_Mais_Molassiotis_2008_pp._2604–2623-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Consumption is sometimes advocated as a treatment for <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_allergies" class="mw-redirect" title="Seasonal allergies">seasonal allergies</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, but scientific evidence to support the claim is inconclusive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mayo&#95;125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayo-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey is generally considered ineffective for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Allergic_conjunctivitis" title="Allergic conjunctivitis">allergic conjunctivitis</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mayo&#95;125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayo-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rudmik&#95;Hoy&#95;Schlosser&#95;Harvey&#95;2012&#95;pp.&#95;281–298&#95;127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rudmik_Hoy_Schlosser_Harvey_2012_pp._281–298-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of calories in honey are from fructose. When consumed in addition to a normal diet, fructose causes significant weight gain, but when fructose was substituted for other carbohydrates of equal energy value there was no effect on body weight.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-aim&#95;128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aim-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Honey has a mild laxative effect which has been noted as being helpful in alleviating constipation and bloating.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Health_hazards">Health hazards</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Health hazards"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey is generally safe when taken in typical food amounts,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Goldman2014&#95;118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman2014-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-mayo&#95;125-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayo-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but it may have various, potential <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect" title="Adverse effect">adverse effects</a> or <a href="/wiki/Drug_interaction" title="Drug interaction">interactions</a> in combination with excessive consumption, existing <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a> conditions, or <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drugs</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mayo&#95;125-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayo-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Included among these are mild reactions to high intake, such as <a href="/wiki/Anxiety_(mood)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anxiety (mood)">anxiety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Hyperactivity" title="Hyperactivity">hyperactivity</a> in about 10% of children, according to one study.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Goldman2014&#95;118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman2014-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> No symptoms of anxiety, insomnia, or hyperactivity were detected with honey consumption compared to <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a>, according to another study.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Goldman2014&#95;118-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman2014-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey consumption may interact adversely with existing <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergies</a>, high <a href="/wiki/Blood_sugar" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood sugar">blood sugar</a> levels (as in <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>), or <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulant" title="Anticoagulant">anticoagulants</a> used to control <a href="/wiki/Bleeding" title="Bleeding">bleeding</a>, among other <a href="/wiki/Clinical_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical medicine">clinical</a> conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mayo&#95;125-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayo-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>People who have a <a href="/wiki/Immunocompromised" class="mw-redirect" title="Immunocompromised">weakened immune system</a> may be at risk of bacterial or fungal infection from eating honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-safety&#95;130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safety-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Botulism">Botulism</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Botulism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Infants can develop <a href="/wiki/Botulism" title="Botulism">botulism</a> after consuming honey contaminated with <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_botulinum" title="Clostridium botulinum">Clostridium botulinum</a></i> endospores.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NHB&#95;botulism&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHB_botulism-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While the risk honey poses to infant health is small, taking the risk is not recommended until after one year of age, and then giving honey is considered safe.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ENY128&#95;132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENY128-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Infantile botulism shows geographical variation. In the UK, only six cases were reported between 1976 and 2006,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FSA&#95;botulism&#95;133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FSA_botulism-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> yet the US has much higher rates: 1.9 per 100,000 live births, 47.2% of which are in California.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CdcBotulism&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CdcBotulism-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Toxic_honey">Toxic honey</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Toxic honey"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mad_honey" title="Mad honey">Mad honey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bees_and_toxic_chemicals#Toxic_honey" title="Bees and toxic chemicals">Bees and toxic chemicals §&#160;Toxic honey</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mad_honey_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Mad honey disease">Mad honey intoxication</a> is a result of eating honey containing <a href="/wiki/Grayanotoxin" title="Grayanotoxin">grayanotoxins</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JansenKleerekooper2012&#95;135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JansenKleerekooper2012-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Honey produced from flowers of <a href="/wiki/Rhododendron" title="Rhododendron">rhododendrons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalmia_latifolia" title="Kalmia latifolia">mountain laurels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalmia_angustifolia" title="Kalmia angustifolia">sheep laurel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Azalea" title="Azalea">azaleas</a> may cause honey intoxication. Symptoms include dizziness, weakness, excessive perspiration, nausea, and vomiting. Less commonly, low blood pressure, shock, heart rhythm irregularities, and convulsions may occur, with rare cases resulting in death. According to the FDA, honey intoxication is more likely when using "natural" unprocessed honey from farmers who may have a small number of hives because commercial processing, which pools of honey from numerous sources, dilutes the toxins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FDA&#95;136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDA-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Toxic honey may also result when bees are proximate to <a href="/wiki/Tutu_(plant)" title="Tutu (plant)">tutu</a> bushes (<i>Coriaria arborea</i>) and the vine hopper insect (<i><a href="/wiki/Scolypopa_australis" title="Scolypopa australis">Scolypopa australis</a></i>). Both are found throughout New Zealand. Bees gather honeydew produced by the vine hopper insects feeding on the tutu plant. This introduces the poison <a href="/wiki/Tutin_(toxin)" title="Tutin (toxin)">tutin</a> into honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NBA&#95;137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NBA-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Only a few areas in New Zealand (the <a href="/wiki/Coromandel_Peninsula" title="Coromandel Peninsula">Coromandel Peninsula</a>, Eastern <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Plenty_Region" title="Bay of Plenty Region">Bay of Plenty Region</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Marlborough_Sounds" title="Marlborough Sounds">Marlborough Sounds</a>) frequently produce toxic honey. Symptoms of tutin poisoning include vomiting, delirium, giddiness, increased excitability, stupor, coma, and violent convulsions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To reduce the risk of tutin poisoning, humans should not eat honey taken from feral hives in the risk areas of New Zealand. Since December 2001, New Zealand beekeepers have been required to reduce the risk of producing toxic honey by closely monitoring tutu, vine hopper, and foraging conditions within 3&#160;km (2&#160;mi) of their apiary.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="needed for section in general (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Intoxication is rarely dangerous.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JansenKleerekooper2012&#95;135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JansenKleerekooper2012-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Folk_medicine">Folk medicine</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Folk medicine"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Folk_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk medicine">folk medicine</a>, honey was used both orally and topically to treat various ailments including <a href="/wiki/Gastric" class="mw-redirect" title="Gastric">gastric</a> disturbances, <a href="/wiki/Ulcer" title="Ulcer">ulcers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a> <a href="/wiki/Wound" title="Wound">wounds</a>, and skin <a href="/wiki/Burn" title="Burn">burns</a> by ancient Greeks and Egyptians, and in <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pećanac-&#95;139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pećanac--139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cueva_arana.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Cueva_arana.svg/250px-Cueva_arana.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="299" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Cueva_arana.svg/330px-Cueva_arana.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Cueva_arana.svg/500px-Cueva_arana.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="341" data-file-height="600" /></a><figcaption>Honey seeker depicted in an 8000-year-old cave painting at <a href="/wiki/Cuevas_de_la_Ara%C3%B1a_en_Bicorp" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuevas de la Araña en Bicorp">Coves de L'Aranya, Bicorp</a> in València</figcaption></figure>
<p>Honey collection is an ancient activity,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crane83&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crane83-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> long preceding the honey bee's domestication; this traditional practice is known as <a href="/wiki/Honey_hunting" title="Honey hunting">honey hunting</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> rock painting in a cave in <a href="/wiki/Valencian_Community" title="Valencian Community">Valencia</a>, Spain, dating back at least 8,000 years, depicts two honey foragers collecting honey and honeycomb from a wild bees' nest. The figures are depicted carrying baskets or gourds, and using a ladder or series of ropes to reach the nest.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crane83&#95;11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crane83-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Humans followed the <a href="/wiki/Greater_honeyguide" title="Greater honeyguide">greater honeyguide</a> bird to wild beehives;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> this behavior may have evolved with early hominids.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The oldest known honey remains were found in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> during the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Baku%E2%80%93Tbilisi%E2%80%93Ceyhan_pipeline" title="Baku–Tbilisi–Ceyhan pipeline">Baku–Tbilisi–Ceyhan pipeline</a>: archaeologists found honey remains on the inner surface of clay vessels unearthed in an ancient tomb, dating back between 4,700 and 5,500 years.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In ancient Georgia, several types of honey were buried with a person for journeys into the afterlife, including linden, berry, and meadow-flower varieties.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The first written records of beekeeping are from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (April 2023)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, where honey was used to sweeten cakes, biscuits, and other foods and as a base for <a href="/wiki/Unguents" class="mw-redirect" title="Unguents">unguents</a> in Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Hieroglyphs" class="mw-redirect" title="Hieroglyphs">hieroglyphs</a>. The dead were often buried in or with honey in Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and other regions. Bees were kept at temples to produce honey for temple offerings, mummification and other uses.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-med&#95;148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-med-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In southern <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a> (present day <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>), the Iron Age <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrian</a> tribe of the <a href="/wiki/Abroi" title="Abroi">Abroi</a> were known for preparing <a href="/wiki/Mead" title="Mead">mead</a>, a wine from honey, as documented by <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">Hecataeus of Miletus</a> in the 6th century BCE.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>, honey was produced from the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic periods</a>. In 594&#160;BCE,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> beekeeping around <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> was so widespread that <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">Solon</a> passed a law about it: "He who sets up hives of bees must put them 300 feet [90 metres] away from those already installed by another".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-crane1999&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crane1999-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Greek archaeological excavations of pottery located ancient hives.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Princeton&#95;University&#95;Press&#95;152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Press-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Columella" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucius Columella">Columella</a>, Greek beekeepers of the Hellenistic period did not hesitate to move their hives over rather long distances to maximize production, taking advantage of the different vegetative cycles in different regions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Princeton&#95;University&#95;Press&#95;152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Press-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The spiritual and supposed therapeutic use of honey in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient India">ancient India</a> was documented in both the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a> texts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pećanac-&#95;139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pećanac--139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Religious_significance">Religious significance</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Religious significance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">ancient Greek religion</a>, the food of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> and the twelve <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Olympians" title="Twelve Olympians">Gods of Olympus</a> was honey in the form of nectar and <a href="/wiki/Ambrosia" title="Ambrosia">ambrosia</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">Promised Land</a> (Canaan, the Land of Israel) is described 16 times as "the <a href="/wiki/Exodus_3" class="mw-redirect" title="Exodus 3">land of milk and honey</a>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1stANE&#95;154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1stANE-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as a metaphor for its bounty. Of the 55 times the word "honey" appears in the Hebrew Bible, 16 are part of the expression "the land of milk and honey", and only twice is "honey" explicitly associated with bees, both being related to wild bees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1stANE&#95;154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1stANE-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Modern biblical researchers long considered that the original Hebrew word used in the Bible, (דבש, <i>devash)</i>, refers to the <a href="/wiki/Date_honey" title="Date honey">sweet syrup</a> produced from <a href="/wiki/Fig" title="Fig">figs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_dactylifera" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoenix dactylifera">dates</a>, because the domestication of the honey bee was completely undocumented through archaeology anywhere in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">ancient Near East</a> (excluding Egypt) at the time associated with the earlier biblical narratives<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1stANE&#95;154-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1stANE-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (books of <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Exodus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Judges" title="Book of Judges">Judges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">Kings</a>, etc.). In 2005, however, an apiary dating from the 10th century BC was found in <a href="/wiki/Tel_Rehov" title="Tel Rehov">Tel Rehov</a>, Israel that contained 100 hives, estimated to produce half a ton of honey annually.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-1stANE&#95;154-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1stANE-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This was, as of 2007, the only such finding made by archaeologists in the entire ancient Near East region, and it opens the possibility that biblical honey was indeed bee honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1stANE&#95;154-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1stANE-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:May_you_all_have_a_sweet_year_(365-271)_(6194230350).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/May_you_all_have_a_sweet_year_%28365-271%29_%286194230350%29.jpg/250px-May_you_all_have_a_sweet_year_%28365-271%29_%286194230350%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="178" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/May_you_all_have_a_sweet_year_%28365-271%29_%286194230350%29.jpg/500px-May_you_all_have_a_sweet_year_%28365-271%29_%286194230350%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="4622" data-file-height="3300" /></a><figcaption>In <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, honey symbolizes the sweetness of the New Year, <i><a href="/wiki/Rosh_Hashanah" title="Rosh Hashanah">Rosh Hashanah</a></i>, and is traditionally eaten with apple slices.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In Jewish tradition, honey is a symbol for the new year, <i><a href="/wiki/Rosh_Hashanah" title="Rosh Hashanah">Rosh Hashanah</a></i>. At the traditional meal for that holiday, apple slices are dipped in honey and eaten to bring a sweet new year. Some <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> greetings show honey and an apple, symbolizing the feast. In some congregations, small straws of honey are given out to usher in the new year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pure honey is considered <a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">kosher</a> (permitted to be eaten by religious Jews), though it is produced by a flying insect, a non-kosher creature; eating other products of non-kosher animals is forbidden.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It belongs among the <i>parve</i> (neutral) foods, containing neither meat nor dairy products and allowed to be eaten together with either.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christians</a> used honey as a symbol of spiritual perfection in christening ceremonies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-med&#95;148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-med-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Islam, an entire chapter (<a href="/wiki/Surah" title="Surah">Surah</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> is called <i><a href="/wiki/An-Nahl" title="An-Nahl">an-Nahl</a></i> (the Bees). According to his teachings (<i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> strongly recommended <a href="/wiki/Prophetic_medicine" title="Prophetic medicine">honey for healing purposes</a>.The Quran promotes honey as a nutritious and healthy food, saying:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>And thy Lord taught the Bee to build its cells in hills, on trees, and in (men's) habitations; Then to eat of all the produce (of the earth), and find with skill the spacious paths of its Lord: there issues from within their bodies a drink of varying colours, wherein is healing for men: verily in this is a Sign for those who give thought.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-&#39;Abdullah&#95;Yusuf&#95;&#39;Ali&#95;159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;Abdullah_Yusuf_&#39;Ali-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In Hinduism, honey (<i><a href="/wiki/Madhu" title="Madhu">Madhu</a></i>) is one of the five elixirs of life (<i><a href="/wiki/Panchamrita" title="Panchamrita">Panchamrita</a></i>). In temples, honey is poured over the deities in a ritual called <i>Madhu <a href="/wiki/Abhisheka" title="Abhisheka">abhisheka</a></i>. The <i>Vedas</i> and other ancient literature mention the use of honey as a great medicinal and health food.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-fjnet.com&#95;160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fjnet.com-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Buddhism, honey plays an important role in the festival of <i><a href="/wiki/Madhu_Purnima" title="Madhu Purnima">Madhu Purnima</a></i>, celebrated in India and Bangladesh. The day commemorates <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" class="mw-redirect" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha's</a> making peace among his disciples by retreating into the wilderness. According to legend, while he was there a <a href="/wiki/Old_World_monkey" title="Old World monkey">monkey</a> brought him honey to eat. On <i>Madhu Purnima</i>, Buddhists remember this act by giving honey to <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_monasticism" title="Buddhist monasticism">monks</a>. The monkey's gift is frequently depicted in <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_art" title="Buddhist art">Buddhist art</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-fjnet.com&#95;160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fjnet.com-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Popular_culture">Popular culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Honey is especially associated with <a href="/wiki/Winnie-the-Pooh" title="Winnie-the-Pooh">Winnie-the-Pooh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bamse" title="Bamse">Bamse</a>'s thunder honey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="See_also">See also</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Honey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<li><a href="/wiki/Mellivory" title="Mellivory">Mellivory</a>&#160;– Consumption of honey</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Monofloral_honey" title="Monofloral honey">Monofloral honey</a>&#160;– Honey from the nectar of one plant species</li>
